<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genome sequence of Gossypium herbaceum and genome updates of Gossypium arboreum and Gossypium hirsutum provide insights into cotton A-genome evolution"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2020 52:5"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-04-13"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Upon assembling the first Gossypium herbaceum (A1) genome and substantially improving the existing Gossypium arboreum (A2) and Gossypium hirsutum ((AD)1) genomes, we showed that all existing A-genomes may have originated from a common ancestor, referred to here as A0, which was more phylogenetically related to A1 than A2. Further, allotetraploid formation was shown to have preceded the speciation of A1 and A2. Both A-genomes evolved independently, with no ancestor&#8211;progeny relationship. Gaussian probability density function analysis indicates that several long-terminal-repeat bursts that occurred from 5.7&#8201;million years ago to less than 0.61&#8201;million years ago contributed compellingly to A-genome size expansion, speciation and evolution. Abundant species-specific structural variations in genic regions changed the expression of many important genes, which may have led to fiber cell improvement in (AD)1. Our findings resolve existing controversial concepts surrounding A-genome origins and provide valuable genomic resources for cotton genetic improvement. Assembly of the first Gossypium herbaceum genome and improved Gossypium arboreum and Gossypium hirsutum genomes provide insights into the phylogenetic relationships and origin history of cotton A-genomes."/>

                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Upon assembling the first <i>Gossypium herbaceum</i> (A<sub>1</sub>) genome and substantially improving the existing <i>Gossypium arboreum</i> (A<sub>2</sub>) and <i>Gossypium hirsutum</i> ((AD)<sub>1</sub>) genomes, we showed that all existing A-genomes may have originated from a common ancestor, referred to here as A<sub>0</sub>, which was more phylogenetically related to A<sub>1</sub> than A<sub>2</sub>. Further, allotetraploid formation was shown to have preceded the speciation of A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub>. Both A-genomes evolved independently, with no ancestor–progeny relationship. Gaussian probability density function analysis indicates that several long-terminal-repeat bursts that occurred from 5.7 million years ago to less than 0.61 million years ago contributed compellingly to A-genome size expansion, speciation and evolution. Abundant species-specific structural variations in genic regions changed the expression of many important genes, which may have led to fiber cell improvement in (AD)<sub>1</sub>. Our findings resolve existing controversial concepts surrounding A-genome origins and provide valuable genomic resources for cotton genetic improvement.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Cultivated cotton is one of the most economically important crop plants in the world. The allotetraploid Upland cotton, <i>G. hirsutum</i> (<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 26, (AD)<sub>1</sub>), currently dominates the world’s cotton commerce<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Wu, Z. et al. Cotton functional genomics reveals global insight into genome evolution and fiber development. J. Genet. Genomics 44, 511–518 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d31216e609">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Ma, Z. et al. Resequencing a core collection of upland cotton identifies genomic variation and loci influencing fiber quality and yield. Nat. Genet. 50, 803–813 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d31216e612">2</a></sup>. Hybridization between the Old World A-genome progenitor and a New World D-genome ancestor, followed by chromosome doubling, formed the allopolyploid cotton ~1−2 million years ago (Ma)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Senchina, D. S. et al. Rate variation among nuclear genes and the age of polyploidy in Gossypium. Mol. Biol. Evol. 20, 633–643 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d31216e617">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Webber, J. M. Cytogenetic notes on cotton and cotton relatives. II. Science 84, 378 (1936)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d31216e620">4</a></sup>. Uncertainty regarding the actual A-genome donor of the widely cultivated allotetraploid cotton <i>G. hirsutum</i> has persisted<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zahn, L. M. Unraveling the origin of cotton. Science 335, 1148 (2012)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d31216e627">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Stephens, S. G. Phenogenetic evidence for the amphidiploid origin of New World cottons. Nature 153, 53–54 (1944)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d31216e627_1">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hutchinson, J. B., Silow, R. A. &amp; Stephens, S. G. (eds) The Evolution of Gossypium and the Differentiation of the Cultivated Cottons (Oxford Univ. Press, 1947)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d31216e627_2">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Gerstel, D. Chromosomal translocations in interspecific hybrids of the genus Gossypium. Evolution 7, 234–244 (1953)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d31216e627_3">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Palmer, S. A. et al. Archaeogenomic evidence of punctuated genome evolution in Gossypium. Mol. Biol. Evol. 29, 2031–2038 (2012)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d31216e627_4">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hu, Y. et al. Gossypium barbadense and Gossypium hirsutum genomes provide insights into the origin and evolution of allotetraploid cotton. Nat. Genet. 51, 739–748 (2019)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d31216e627_5">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wendel, J. F., Brubaker, C., Alvarez, I., Cronn, R. &amp; Stewart, J. M. Genetics and Genomics of Cotton Vol. 3 (ed. Paterson, A. H.) Ch. 1 (Springer, 2009)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d31216e627_6">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Endrizzi, J. E., Turcotte, E. L. &amp; Kohel, R. J. Genetics, cytology, and evolution of Gossypium. Adv. Genet. 23, 271–375 (1985)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d31216e627_7">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wendel, J. F. New World tetraploid cottons contain Old World cytoplasm. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 86, 4132–4136 (1989)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d31216e630">13</a></sup>. A<sub>1</sub> (<i>n</i> = <i>x</i> = 13) and A<sub>2</sub> (<i>n</i> = <i>x</i> = 13), commonly known as African and Asiatic cotton, respectively, are the only two extant diploid A-genome species in the world<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kulkarni, V. N., Khadi, B. M., Maralappanavar, M. S., Deshapande, L. A. &amp; Narayanan, S. S. Genetics and Genomics of Cotton Vol. 3 (ed. Paterson, A. H.) Ch. 4 (Springer, 2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d31216e651">14</a></sup>. Stephens first proposed in <i>Nature</i>, using genetic and morphological evidence, that A<sub>2</sub> was the A-genome donor of present-day allopolyploid cottons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Stephens, S. G. Phenogenetic evidence for the amphidiploid origin of New World cottons. Nature 153, 53–54 (1944)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d31216e661">6</a></sup>. Gerstel argued via cytogenetic studies that A<sub>1</sub> was more closely related to the A-genome in the allopolyploids than A<sub>2</sub> (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Gerstel, D. Chromosomal translocations in interspecific hybrids of the genus Gossypium. Evolution 7, 234–244 (1953)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d31216e669">8</a></sup>). Despite recent efforts to sequence the cotton genomes, including <i>Gossypium</i> <i>raimondii</i> (D<sub>5</sub>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. 44, 1098–1103 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d31216e682">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423–427 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d31216e685">16</a></sup>, A<sub>2</sub> (refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat. Genet. 46, 567–572 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d31216e691">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Du, X. et al. Resequencing of 243 diploid cotton accessions based on an updated A genome identifies the genetic basis of key agronomic traits. Nat. Genet. 50, 796–802 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d31216e694">18</a></sup>), (AD)<sub>1</sub> (refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Hu, Y. et al. Gossypium barbadense and Gossypium hirsutum genomes provide insights into the origin and evolution of allotetraploid cotton. Nat. Genet. 51, 739–748 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d31216e700">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, F. G. et al. Genome sequence of cultivated Upland cotton (Gossypium hirsutum TM-1) provides insights into genome evolution. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 524–530 (2015)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d31216e703">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, T. Z. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531–537 (2015)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d31216e703_1">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Wang, M. et al. Reference genome sequences of two cultivated allotetraploid cottons, Gossypium hirsutum and Gossypium barbadense. Nat. Genet. 51, 224–229 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d31216e706">21</a></sup>) and <i>Gossypium</i> <i>barbadense</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Hu, Y. et al. Gossypium barbadense and Gossypium hirsutum genomes provide insights into the origin and evolution of allotetraploid cotton. Nat. Genet. 51, 739–748 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d31216e716">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Wang, M. et al. Reference genome sequences of two cultivated allotetraploid cottons, Gossypium hirsutum and Gossypium barbadense. Nat. Genet. 51, 224–229 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d31216e719">21</a></sup> ((AD)<sub>2</sub>, a much less cultivated tetraploid cotton), the origin history of the A-genome donor for the tetraploid (AD)<sub>1</sub>-genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Zahn, L. M. Unraveling the origin of cotton. Science 335, 1148 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d31216e727">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wendel, J. F., Brubaker, C., Alvarez, I., Cronn, R. &amp; Stewart, J. M. Genetics and Genomics of Cotton Vol. 3 (ed. Paterson, A. H.) Ch. 1 (Springer, 2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d31216e730">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wendel, J. F. New World tetraploid cottons contain Old World cytoplasm. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 86, 4132–4136 (1989)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d31216e733">13</a></sup> and the extent of divergence between the A-genomes remain elusive<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Hutchinson, J. New evidence on the origin of the Old World cottons. Heredity 8, 225–241 (1954)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d31216e738">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Renny-Byfield, S. et al. Independent domestication of two Old World cotton species. Genome Biol. Evol. 8, 1940–1947 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d31216e741">23</a></sup>. Abundant studies support a <i>Gossypium</i> species resembling D<sub>5</sub> as the D-genome donor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wendel, J. F. New World tetraploid cottons contain Old World cytoplasm. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 86, 4132–4136 (1989)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d31216e750">13</a></sup>, but currently there is no solid evidence to suggest that the actual A-genome donor of tetraploid cottons is either A<sub>2</sub> (refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Stephens, S. G. Phenogenetic evidence for the amphidiploid origin of New World cottons. Nature 153, 53–54 (1944)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d31216e756">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Hutchinson, J. B., Silow, R. A. &amp; Stephens, S. G. (eds) The Evolution of Gossypium and the Differentiation of the Cultivated Cottons (Oxford Univ. Press, 1947)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d31216e759">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Hu, Y. et al. Gossypium barbadense and Gossypium hirsutum genomes provide insights into the origin and evolution of allotetraploid cotton. Nat. Genet. 51, 739–748 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d31216e762">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Li, F. G. et al. Genome sequence of cultivated Upland cotton (Gossypium hirsutum TM-1) provides insights into genome evolution. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 524–530 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d31216e765">19</a></sup>) or A<sub>1</sub> (refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Gerstel, D. Chromosomal translocations in interspecific hybrids of the genus Gossypium. Evolution 7, 234–244 (1953)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d31216e772">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Palmer, S. A. et al. Archaeogenomic evidence of punctuated genome evolution in Gossypium. Mol. Biol. Evol. 29, 2031–2038 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d31216e775">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wendel, J. F., Brubaker, C., Alvarez, I., Cronn, R. &amp; Stewart, J. M. Genetics and Genomics of Cotton Vol. 3 (ed. Paterson, A. H.) Ch. 1 (Springer, 2009)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d31216e778">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Endrizzi, J. E., Turcotte, E. L. &amp; Kohel, R. J. Genetics, cytology, and evolution of Gossypium. Adv. Genet. 23, 271–375 (1985)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d31216e778_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wendel, J. F. New World tetraploid cottons contain Old World cytoplasm. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 86, 4132–4136 (1989)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d31216e781">13</a></sup>) as has been suggested.</p><p>In this study, we assembled A<sub>1</sub> variety <i>africanum</i> for the first time and re-assembled high-quality A<sub>2</sub> cultivar Shixiya1 and (AD)<sub>1</sub> genetic standard Texas Marker-1 (TM-1) genomes on the basis of PacBio long reads, paired-end sequencing and high-throughput chromosome conformation capture (Hi-C) technologies. Upon assembling and updating cotton genomes, we revealed the origin of cotton A-genomes, the occurrence of several transposable element (TE) bursts and the genetic divergence of diploid A-genomes worldwide. Also, we identified abundant structural variations (SVs) that have affected the expression of neighboring genes and help explain phenotypic differences among the cotton species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing and assembly of three high-quality cotton genomes</h3><p>Here we sequenced the A<sub>1</sub>-genome var. <i>africanum</i> for the first time by generating ~225-gigabase (Gb) PacBio single-molecule real-time (SMRT) long reads (the N50 (minimum length to cover 50% of the total length) of these reads was 13 kilobases (kb)) with 138-fold genome coverage. We generated an assembly that captured 1,556 megabases (Mb) of genome sequences, consisting of 1,781 contigs with the N50 of these contigs reaching up to 1,915 kb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Tab1">1</a>). The initial assemblies were then corrected by using highly accurate Illumina paired-end reads (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM1">1</a>). Finally, 95.69% of total contigs spanning 1,489 Mb were categorized and ordered into 13 chromosome-scale scaffolds using Hi-C data (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Assembly and annotation of A<sub>1</sub>-, A<sub>2</sub>- and (AD)<sub>1</sub>-genomes in the current and two previous studies</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Also, the A<sub>2</sub>-genome cultivar Shixiya1 and the (AD)<sub>1</sub>-genome accession TM-1 were further sequenced using high-depth SMRT long reads resulting in 177-fold A<sub>2</sub>-genome coverage (~310 Gb) and 81.6-fold (AD)<sub>1</sub>-genome coverage (~205 Gb), respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM1">1</a>). The total assembled genome size for A<sub>2</sub> was 1,637 Mb with 92.18% (1,509 Mb) of all sequences oriented and organized into 13 chromosomes. The resulting assembled genome size for (AD)<sub>1</sub> was 2,290 Mb with 99.17% of all sequences anchored on 26 chromosomes (A<sub>t1</sub>, 1,449 Mb; D<sub>t1</sub>, 822 Mb). Compared with a recent PacBio-based A<sub>2</sub> assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Du, X. et al. Resequencing of 243 diploid cotton accessions based on an updated A genome identifies the genetic basis of key agronomic traits. Nat. Genet. 50, 796–802 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d31216e998">18</a></sup> (8,223 contigs with an N50 of 1,100 kb), our assembly consists of 2,432 contigs with N50 of 1,832 kb, resulting in a reduced number of gaps from 3,707 to 1,163 (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Tab1">1</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig1">1a</a>). The N50 of our updated (AD)<sub>1</sub>-genome is 5,020 kb (1,892 kb reported in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Wang, M. et al. Reference genome sequences of two cultivated allotetraploid cottons, Gossypium hirsutum and Gossypium barbadense. Nat. Genet. 51, 224–229 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d31216e1011">21</a></sup>), with significantly fewer gaps compared with the most recently published genome (893 gaps versus 2,564 gaps reported in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Wang, M. et al. Reference genome sequences of two cultivated allotetraploid cottons, Gossypium hirsutum and Gossypium barbadense. Nat. Genet. 51, 224–229 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d31216e1015">21</a></sup>), which represents ~2.65-fold improvement (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Tab1">1</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig1">1b</a>). Our assembled cotton genomes showed high congruence because the strongest signals from the Hi-C data clustered at the expected diagonal (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig6">1</a>). Collinear relationships existed in quantity among cotton genomes, indicating that our pseudo-chromosomes derived from anchored and oriented contigs are of high quality (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig7">2</a>). Our (AD)<sub>1</sub>-genome assembly also shared a high consistency for each chromosome with the previously published genetic map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Wang, S. et al. Sequence-based ultra-dense genetic and physical maps reveal structural variations of allopolyploid cotton genomes. Genome Biol. 16, 108 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d31216e1034">24</a></sup> (Pearson correlation coefficients &gt; 0.98) (Extended Data Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig8">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig9">4</a>). These updated A<sub>2</sub>- and (AD)<sub>1</sub>-genomes may supplant earlier assemblies as chromosome-scale references.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Distribution of genomic components of A1 and A2 across chromosomes and chromosomal variant events within the Gossypium lineage."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Distribution of genomic components of A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> across chromosomes and chromosomal variant events within the <i>Gossypium</i> lineage.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0607-4/MediaObjects/41588_2020_607_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0607-4/MediaObjects/41588_2020_607_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="819" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>,<b>b</b>, Statistics of gap numbers in the assembly of A<sub>2</sub>- (<b>a</b>) and (AD)<sub>1</sub>- (<b>b</b>) genomes. A<sub>2</sub>*, previously released A<sub>2</sub>-genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Du, X. et al. Resequencing of 243 diploid cotton accessions based on an updated A genome identifies the genetic basis of key agronomic traits. Nat. Genet. 50, 796–802 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d31216e1084">18</a></sup>; A<sub>t1</sub>* and D<sub>t1</sub>* represent the A<sub>t1</sub>- and D<sub>t1</sub>-subgenome, respectively, of recently released (AD)<sub>1</sub>-genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Wang, M. et al. Reference genome sequences of two cultivated allotetraploid cottons, Gossypium hirsutum and Gossypium barbadense. Nat. Genet. 51, 224–229 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d31216e1099">21</a></sup>. <b>c</b>, Multi-dimensional display of genomic components of A<sub>1</sub>- and A<sub>2</sub>-genomes. The density was calculated per 1 Mb. I, the 13 chromosomes; II, gene density; III−V, coverage by TE, Gypsy and Copia, respectively; VI−VIII, transcriptional state in the ovule at 10 DPA and in root and leaf tissue, respectively. Transcript levels were estimated based on the average depth of mapped RNA reads in nonoverlapping 1-Mb windows. IX, GC content. <b>d</b>, Characterization of genomic variations in <i>Gossypium</i>. Genic synteny blocks are connected by gray lines. Reciprocal translocations and two large inversions are highlighted by dark gray and red links, respectively. <b>e</b>, Synteny maps using whole-genome alignments show that the inversion in chromosome 10 exists in either A<sub>1</sub> or A<sub>t1</sub>, whereas the one in chromosome 12 is found only in A<sub>1</sub>. Genomic homologous blocks ≥ 20 kb are drawn in the plots. Chr, chromosome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The A<sub>1</sub>-, A<sub>2</sub>- and (AD)<sub>1</sub>-genomes comprise 43,952, 43,278 and 74,350 annotated protein-coding genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM1">2</a>), respectively, mainly in both ends of the chromosomes because as much as 79.71% of A<sub>1</sub>, 80.06% of A<sub>2</sub> and 64.09% of the (AD)<sub>1</sub>-genome are composed of TEs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM1">3</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig1">1c</a>). Also, TE-rich regions in the middle region of chromosomes remain silent, with low transcript levels, in contrast to gene-rich regions at both ends of chromosomes with high transcript levels (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig1">1c</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Chromosomal translocation and inversions within <i>Gossypium</i> lineage</h3><p>Compared with that of A<sub>1</sub>, the genome of A<sub>2</sub> underwent a reciprocal translocation between chromosomes 1 and 2 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig1">1d</a>), which is supported by previous cytogenetic data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Gerstel, D. Chromosomal translocations in interspecific hybrids of the genus Gossypium. Evolution 7, 234–244 (1953)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d31216e1175">8</a></sup>. This translocation likely occurred after the two species separated and then became fixed in A<sub>2</sub>. The A<sub>1</sub>- and A<sub>2</sub>-genomes differed from the A<sub>t1</sub>-subgenome by two and three translocations, respectively, of which the two reciprocal translocations between chromosomes 2 and 3, and 4 and 5, specifically occurred in the tetraploid A<sub>t1</sub>-subgenome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig1">1d</a>), suggesting that these translocations probably occurred after polyploidization. The two translocations that specially occurred in A<sub>t1</sub> were also confirmed by multivalent formations in hybrids between the allotetraploids and A<sub>1</sub> or A<sub>2</sub> (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Menzel, M. Y. &amp; Brown, M. S. The significance of multivalent formation in three-species Gossypium hybrids. Genetics 39, 546–557 (1954)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d31216e1200">25</a></sup>). Two large-scale inversion events were detected between A<sub>1</sub>- and A<sub>2</sub>-genomes in chromosomes 10 and 12 that were confirmed by Hi-C data and also by PCR amplifications (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig10">5</a>). The collinear relationship analysis of these cotton genomes indicated that the inversion in chromosome 12 specifically occurred in A<sub>1</sub> with the syntenic blocks inverted at the diagonal between ~15.96 Mb and ~77.61 Mb; the inversion in chromosome 10 may have occurred either in A<sub>1</sub> at the diagonal between ~18.4 Mb and ~61.3 Mb or in A<sub>t1</sub> between ~23.09 Mb and ~97.42 Mb (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig1">1e</a>). Thus, the two inversions likely occurred after the speciation of A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> cottons.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Origin of allotetraploid cotton</h3><p>A molecular tree based on single-copy genes suggests that the common ancestor of the A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> clade was phylogenetically a sister to the A<sub>t</sub>-subgenomes (A<sub>t1</sub> and A<sub>t2</sub>) of (AD)<sub>1</sub> and of (AD)<sub>2</sub>, respectively, and the divergence time for A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> was estimated to be ~0.7 Ma (0.4−1.4 Ma), well after the allotetraploid formation ~1.0−1.6 Ma (the values for the separation of A<sub>t</sub> to A<sub>1</sub> or A<sub>2</sub>, and D<sub>t</sub> to D<sub>5</sub>) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig2">2a</a>). Gene trees with specific recombination regions also supported the sister relationships between the A<sub>1</sub>–A<sub>2</sub> clade and A<sub>t1</sub> (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig11">6a,b</a>). Whole-genome phylogenetic analysis showed that the major topology 1 (A<sub>t1</sub>, 56.17%; A<sub>t2</sub>, 59.75%) supported the constructed species tree in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig2">2a</a>. The minor topology 2 with the sister relationship of A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>t</sub> (A<sub>t1</sub>, 22.22%; A<sub>t2</sub>, 22.11%) had a slightly higher rate than the other minor topology 3 with the sister relationship of A<sub>2</sub> and A<sub>t</sub> (A<sub>t1</sub>, 21.61%; A<sub>t2</sub>, 18.14%) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig2">2b</a> and Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig11">6c</a>). Synonymous substitution (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) analysis indicated that A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> had the lowest divergence (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values), compared with all other pairs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig2">2c</a>). Likewise, a significantly greater number of identical sites were found between orthologs of A<sub>1</sub> versus A<sub>2</sub> relative to either A<sub>1</sub> or A<sub>2</sub> versus A<sub>t1</sub> or A<sub>t2</sub> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig2">2d</a>). We further selected representative cotton lines, including 30 (AD)<sub>1</sub>, 14 A<sub>1</sub> and 21 A<sub>2</sub> accessions, to construct a phylogenetic tree based on whole-genome SNP studies to further validate the relationships of A<sub>1</sub>, A<sub>2</sub> and A<sub>t1</sub> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig2">2e</a> and Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig12">7</a>). Because the actual A-genome donor may be extinct, we compared A<sub>t1</sub>, A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> accessions with the D<sub>5</sub>, an outgroup for all A-genome species. The distance from D<sub>5</sub> to A<sub>t1</sub> was much smaller than that from D<sub>5</sub> to its previously thought common ancestor, A<sub>1</sub> or A<sub>2</sub>. About 30.54% of the SNPs of A<sub>t1</sub> were identical to the corresponding sites in the D<sub>5</sub>-genome, whereas only 20.52% and 20.04% of ancestral alleles of A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub>, respectively, were identical to the corresponding sites in D<sub>5</sub>-genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig2">2e</a>). The nucleotide variation analysis indicated that A<sub>1</sub> has relatively fewer nucleotide variations than A<sub>2</sub> compared with A<sub>t1</sub> across the 13 chromosomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig2">2f</a>). Based on these evidence, we constructed a revised model in which neither A<sub>1</sub> nor A<sub>2</sub> is the actual A-genome donor. Instead, hybridization between the common ancestor (A<sub>0</sub>) of all A-genomes (A<sub>1</sub>, A<sub>2</sub> and A<sub>t</sub>) and a D<sub>5</sub>-genome resembling <i>G. raimondii</i> formed the allotetraploid cotton (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig2">2g</a>). Our results also indicated that the A<sub>0</sub>, inferred as the possible A<sub>t</sub> donor, was more phylogenetically related to A<sub>1</sub> than A<sub>2</sub>. The AD ((AD)<sub>1</sub> and (AD)<sub>2</sub>) tetraploidization occurred approximately 1.0−1.6 Ma; A<sub>0</sub> then developed into two A-genomes around 0.7 Ma (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig2">2h</a>). The finding that A<sub>0</sub> is a common ancestor for A<sub>1</sub>, A<sub>2</sub>, the A<sub>t1</sub>-subgenome in (AD)<sub>1</sub> and the A<sub>t2</sub>-subgenome in (AD)<sub>2</sub> resolves a puzzle regarding previous inconsistent phylogenetic data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Stephens, S. G. Phenogenetic evidence for the amphidiploid origin of New World cottons. Nature 153, 53–54 (1944)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d31216e1457">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hutchinson, J. B., Silow, R. A. &amp; Stephens, S. G. (eds) The Evolution of Gossypium and the Differentiation of the Cultivated Cottons (Oxford Univ. Press, 1947)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d31216e1457_1">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Gerstel, D. Chromosomal translocations in interspecific hybrids of the genus Gossypium. Evolution 7, 234–244 (1953)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d31216e1457_2">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Palmer, S. A. et al. Archaeogenomic evidence of punctuated genome evolution in Gossypium. Mol. Biol. Evol. 29, 2031–2038 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d31216e1460">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wendel, J. F., Brubaker, C., Alvarez, I., Cronn, R. &amp; Stewart, J. M. Genetics and Genomics of Cotton Vol. 3 (ed. Paterson, A. H.) Ch. 1 (Springer, 2009)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d31216e1463">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Endrizzi, J. E., Turcotte, E. L. &amp; Kohel, R. J. Genetics, cytology, and evolution of Gossypium. Adv. Genet. 23, 271–375 (1985)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d31216e1463_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wendel, J. F. New World tetraploid cottons contain Old World cytoplasm. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 86, 4132–4136 (1989)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d31216e1466">13</a></sup> and explains why interspecific hybridization of A<sub>1</sub> or A<sub>2</sub> with D<sub>5</sub> is often unsuccessful, because the genetic distances between the current A- and D-genomes are great enough to preclude fertilization.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="The evolution of the allotetraploid cotton genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: The evolution of the allotetraploid cotton genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0607-4/MediaObjects/41588_2020_607_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0607-4/MediaObjects/41588_2020_607_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="894" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Inferred phylogenetic analysis among <i>Gossypium</i> and other eudicot plants. <b>b</b>, Summary of phylogenetic analysis with the approximately unbiased test in 10-kb windows. <b>c</b>, Distribution of <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values for orthologous genes among cotton genomes. Peak values for each comparison are indicated in the parentheses. <b>d</b>, Comparisons of identical sites in orthologous genes. Violin plots summarize the distribution of identical sites. The center line in each box indicates the median, and the box limits indicate the upper and lower quartiles of divergence (<i>n</i> = 20 types of synonymous mutation). <i>P</i> values were derived with Student’s <i>t</i>-test. <b>e</b>, Phylogenetic and ancestral allele analysis based on SNPs. The red, blue and green triangles represent the collapsed 21 A<sub>2</sub> accessions, 14 A<sub>1</sub> accessions and 30 (AD)<sub>1</sub> accessions, respectively. The percentage value indicates the percentage of ancestral alleles for each species that were identical to those of the D<sub>5</sub>-genome. <b>f</b>, Number of nucleotide variations in A<sub>1</sub> or A<sub>2</sub> compared with A<sub>t1</sub> across the chromosomes. <b>g</b>, A model for the formation of allotetraploid cotton showing fiber phenotypes from the (AD)<sub>1</sub> (accession TM-1), the D<sub>5</sub>, the A<sub>1</sub> (var. <i>africanum</i>) and the A<sub>2</sub> (cv. Shixiya1). Scale bar, 5 mm. <b>h</b>, A schematic map of the evolution of cotton genomes. Major evolutionary events are shown in dashed boxes.</p><p><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM4">Source data</a></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Population genomic study of two A-genome species</h3><p>We collected 14 A<sub>1</sub> and 67 A<sub>2</sub> representative cotton accessions from India, Pakistan, China and other countries to study the genetic divergence between A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig3">3a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM1">4</a>). All resequencing reads with an average coverage depth of ~7.2× for each accession were mapped to our assembled A<sub>2</sub>-genome for SNP identification. A total of 11,652,404 SNPs and 1,716,908 indels (ranging from 1 to 259 base pairs (bp) in length) were identified (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM1">5</a>). Principal component analysis (PCA) based on SNPs showed that, despite their geographic origins, these cotton accessions were clustered in two independent groups: the A<sub>1</sub> group and A<sub>2</sub> group (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig3">3b</a>). The neighbor-joining tree using SNPs indicated that A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> clustered in two independent clades, and A<sub>2</sub> from India and Pakistan and A<sub>2</sub> from China have the closest relatives (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig3">3c</a>), which was confirmed by sliding window phylogenetic analysis with an average weighting of 55% in topology 1 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig3">3d</a>). The topology 2 is nearly identical to topology 3 throughout chromosomes, but there are several weak shifts in support toward topology 2 potentially reflecting the introgression between A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> distributed in China (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig3">3e</a> and Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig13">8</a>). Model-based clustering showed that the population structures of A<sub>1</sub> accessions were obviously significantly different from A<sub>2</sub> accessions (number of clusters (<i>K</i>) = 2), and the population divergence between the A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> from the India and Pakistan group or from China reached almost 1.0, which suggested that these differences clearly distinguish A<sub>2</sub> from A<sub>1</sub> as two cotton species, and may explain the phenomenon in which interspecific hybridizations of A<sub>2</sub> with A<sub>1</sub> are often unsuccessful (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig3">3c,f</a>). Several A<sub>2</sub> accessions from India and Pakistan were clustered sisterly to all A<sub>2</sub> accessions collected from China, and the accessions from China had distinct population structures from accessions from India and Pakistan (<i>K</i> = 3). According to our results and the recorded history of Chinese Asian cotton<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Watt, G. The Wild and Cultivated Cotton Plants of the World (Longmans, Green and Co., 1907)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d31216e1649">26</a></sup>, we concluded that A<sub>2</sub> was likely introduced to China from India and/or Pakistan, then developed into a distinct geographical race (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig3">3c</a>). Two accessions of A<sub>1</sub> var. <i>africanum</i> were gathered at the root of all other A<sub>1</sub> accessions with no obvious impact on A<sub>2</sub> development, which did not support the notion that <i>africanum</i> is the source of both cultivated A<sub>1</sub>- and A<sub>2</sub>-genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kulkarni, V. N., Khadi, B. M., Maralappanavar, M. S., Deshapande, L. A. &amp; Narayanan, S. S. Genetics and Genomics of Cotton Vol. 3 (ed. Paterson, A. H.) Ch. 4 (Springer, 2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d31216e1676">14</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig3">3g</a>). The large genetic differences revealed by population analysis and chromosomal SVs between A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> suggest that two A-genomes were evolved independently, with A<sub>1</sub> var. <i>africanum</i> as the only living ancestor of A<sub>1</sub> accessions.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Geographic distribution and population analysis of the A1 and A2 accessions."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Geographic distribution and population analysis of the A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> accessions.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0607-4/MediaObjects/41588_2020_607_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0607-4/MediaObjects/41588_2020_607_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="939" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Geographic distribution of the collected A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> accessions. Green, red and yellow dots represent A<sub>1</sub> accessions and A<sub>2</sub> accessions collected in China and outside of China, respectively. The map was drawn using the maptools package (<a href="http://maptools.r-forge.r-project.org/">http://maptools.r-forge.r-project.org/</a>). <b>b</b>, PCA plots of the first three components for A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> accessions. Dot colors are the same as in <b>a</b>. <b>c</b>, Analysis of genetic relationship between all A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> accessions. The upper and lower panels show the phylogenetic tree based on whole-genome SNP studies and population structure of all accessions based on different numbers of clusters (<i>K</i> = 2–3), respectively. Branch colors are the same as in <b>a</b>. CHG, A<sub>2</sub> accessions from the China group; IPG, A<sub>2</sub> accessions from the India and Pakistan group. <b>d</b>, Average weightings for the three possible topologies in whole genomes. <b>e</b>, Weightings for all three topologies described in <b>d</b> across chromosome 7 using sliding windows. <b>f</b>, Population divergence (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>) across the three groups described in <b>c</b>. <b>g</b>, Phylogenetic analysis based on SNPs. The yellow and green triangles represent the collapsed 67 A<sub>2</sub> accessions and 12 A<sub>1</sub> accessions, respectively. Two A<sub>1</sub> var. <i>africanum</i> accessions (Ghe01 and Ghe04) gathered at the root of the 12 A<sub>1</sub> accessions. PC1, the first principal component (PC); PC2, the second PC; PC3, the third PC.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genome expansions and evolution</h3><p>Among genome-sequenced plants of the order Malvales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Teh, B. T. et al. The draft genome of tropical fruit durian (Durio zibethinus). Nat. Genet. 49, 1633–1641 (2017)." href="#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d31216e1796">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101–108 (2011)." href="#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d31216e1796_1">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Islam, M. S. et al. Comparative genomics of two jute species and insight into fibre biogenesis. Nat. Plants 3, 16223 (2017)." href="#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d31216e1796_2">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Gao, Y. et al. De novo genome assembly of the red silk cotton tree (Bombax ceiba). GigaScience 7, giy051 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d31216e1799">30</a></sup>, D<sub>5</sub> and the D<sub>t1</sub>-subgenome in (AD)<sub>1</sub> are similar in genome sizes relative to <i>Bombax ceiba</i> or <i>Durio zibethinus</i>, but are expanded nearly twofold compared with the <i>Theobroma cacao</i> and <i>Corchorus capsularis</i> genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig4">4a</a>). The two A-genomes and the A<sub>t1</sub>-subgenome experienced a further twofold expansion that was highly correlated with TE bursts (correlation coefficient, <i>R</i><sup>2</sup> = 0.978) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig4">4a</a>). While both the D<sub>5</sub>-genome (738 Mb) and D<sub>t1</sub>-subgenome (822 Mb) are nearly equivalent in size relative to the <i>D. zibethinus</i> genome (715 Mb), long terminal repeat (LTR) families in <i>Gossypium</i> (52.42% of the D<sub>t1</sub>-subgenome, 53.2% of the D<sub>5</sub>-genome) were greatly expanded in comparison to <i>D. zibethinus</i> (26.2%). As much as 72.57% of the A<sub>1</sub>-genome and 73.62% of the A<sub>2</sub>-genome were composed of LTRs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig4">4b</a>). LTR retrotransposons in <i>Gossypium</i> and <i>B. ceiba</i> have experienced continuing and more recent amplification bursts from 0−2 Ma, while <i>D. zibethinus</i> underwent a distinct amplification burst event around 8−10 Ma (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig4">4c</a>). LTR retrotransposons in the A<sub>2</sub>-genome were further classified into 64 families, of which 68% belonged to the Gypsy superfamily and 12.6% to Copia (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig4">4d</a>). By using representative LTR/Gypsy sequences (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM1">1</a>) to evaluate TE hits in cotton genomes, five distinct insertion peaks for the Gypsy-type LTR with identities from 65−76% to 96.4−99.4% were observed in different cotton genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig4">4e</a>). We used our Gaussian probability density function (GPDF) analysis to estimate the burst time of major peaks, finding that the earliest insertion event occurred ~5.7 Ma, which is the expected speciation time for A- and D-genomes (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig14">9</a> and Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig4">4f</a>). The peak with 85.5−88.5% identity, corresponding to ~2.0 Ma, is found specifically in D<sub>t1</sub>- and A<sub>t1</sub>-genomes, but not in D<sub>5</sub>, A<sub>1</sub> or A<sub>2</sub>, suggesting that the allotetraploid cotton may have formed as early as ~2.0 Ma. The peak with 87−89.5% identity corresponded to 0.89 Ma and is common to both A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub>, indicating that speciation might occur at a later time. Indeed, the 93.0−93.8% identity (or 0.61 Ma) peak is unique to A<sub>1</sub>, and the last peak (with 96.4−99.4% identity; no valid calculation of ages because it is too close to date) is A<sub>2</sub>-specific. Our data showed that A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> speciation occurred 0.89−0.61 Ma. This was confirmed by results (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM1">2a–c</a>, TDIV1 (divergence time between A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub>) = 1,016,499 yr) obtained from <i>fastsimocoal2</i> analysis, which used 30 accessions from (AD)<sub>1</sub>, 14 from A<sub>1</sub> and 21 from A<sub>2</sub>, as reported in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig2">2e</a>. However, G-PhoCS analysis, which used data from the fully assembled A<sub>1</sub>-, A<sub>2</sub>- and (AD)<sub>1</sub>-genomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM1">2d–f</a>), did not quite fit our previous model. We suggest that G-PhoCS may not fit well for evolutionary analysis of genomes with high TE contents, such as cotton.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Genome expansions in sequenced Malvales plants, particularly in cotton, and quantitative and comprehensive analysis of LTRs, especially Gypsy-type."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Genome expansions in sequenced Malvales plants, particularly in cotton, and quantitative and comprehensive analysis of LTRs, especially Gypsy-type.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0607-4/MediaObjects/41588_2020_607_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0607-4/MediaObjects/41588_2020_607_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="864" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Genome size expansion is highly correlated with TE amplification bursts (<i>R</i><sup>2</sup> = 0.978). The red line shows the linear relationship between genome size and TE content. <b>b</b>, Genomic component comparisons among genome-sequenced Malvales plants. <b>c</b>, Analysis of intact LTR numbers and insertion time in Malvales plants. <b>d</b>, Classification of intact LTRs in the A<sub>2</sub>-genome. LTR families with a copy number of ≥100 are shown. <b>e</b>, Identity distribution pattern of TE hits presented as a dot-plot. The most recent LTR/Gypsy sequence of LTR families was selected as the representative sequence for detecting additional TE hits in the genomes. A total of 262,377 dots in D<sub>5</sub>, 585,658 in D<sub>t1</sub>, 3,541,372 in A<sub>t1</sub>, 4,218,810 in A<sub>1</sub> and 5,035,006 in A<sub>2</sub> were drawn in the dot-plot. P1–P5 represent the identified five distinct bursts in different cotton genomes. <b>f</b>, Number of TE hits for the representative sequence and their associated identity values. The estimated burst time based on GPDF fitting of each peak is marked. The five peaks, P1–P5, defined in <b>e</b> are highlighted by shaded gray columns. LINE, long interspersed nuclear elements; SINE, short interspersed nuclear elements.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">SVs and fiber development</h3><p>SVs including large deletions and insertions (&gt;50 bp) are reported to drive important phenotypic variation within species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Sun, S. et al. Extensive intraspecific gene order and gene structural variations between Mo17 and other maize genomes. Nat. Genet. 50, 1289–1295 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2000">31</a></sup>. Here we found that (AD)<sub>1</sub> fiber cells underwent fast elongation reaching up to 30.5 ± 0.7 mm until 30 d post anthesis (DPA), whereas fiber cells in A<sub>1</sub> (14.7 ± 0.7 mm) and A<sub>2</sub> (16.1 ± 0.9 mm) elongated at a slower rate and terminated earlier (~20 DPA) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig5">5a</a>). By comparing two A-genomes with the A-subgenome of (AD)<sub>1</sub>, we identified 39,476 deletion and 21,577 insertion events in A<sub>1</sub>, as well as 40,480 deletion and 20,903 insertion events in A<sub>2</sub>. Meanwhile, we obtained 35,997 common SVs events including 21,431 deletions and 14,566 insertions in A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub>, suggesting that these SVs occurred mainly at the common ancestor stage of two A-genome species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig5">5b</a>). Of the total common SVs, 11,395 events (31.66%) were overlapped with genic regions affecting 9,839 unique genes, with 912 events occurring in coding DNA sequences (CDSs), 1,105 in introns and 9,378 in up-/downstream regions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig5">5c</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM3">6</a>). Of the reported 1,753 associated loci for fiber traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Ma, Z. et al. Resequencing a core collection of upland cotton identifies genomic variation and loci influencing fiber quality and yield. Nat. Genet. 50, 803–813 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2034">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Fang, L. et al. Genomic analyses in cotton identify signatures of selection and loci associated with fiber quality and yield traits. Nat. Genet. 49, 1089–1098 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2037">32</a></sup>, 460 associated loci contained common SVs, with those in up-/downstream regions as the major type (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM3">7</a>). We identified 1,545 upregulated and 1,908 downregulated genes by comparing transcriptomes of rapidly elongating fiber cells from the A<sub>t1</sub>-subgenome with those of A<sub>2</sub> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM3">8</a>). Also, 2,941 upregulated and 3,350 downregulated genes were identified with A<sub>t1</sub> and A<sub>1</sub> comparisons at elongating fibers (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM3">9</a>). Of these differentially expressed genes, 949 for A<sub>t1</sub> versus A<sub>2</sub> and 1,687 for A<sub>t1</sub> versus A<sub>1</sub> contained common SVs, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig5">5d</a>, Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig15">10</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM3">10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM3">11</a>). Gene ontology enrichment analysis indicated that fatty acid biosynthesis, cell wall deposition or biogenesis, and carbohydrate metabolism were the most enriched biological processes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig5">5e</a>). Quantitative PCR with reverse transcription (RT–qPCR) analysis of several key genes related to fatty acid biosynthesis, including encoding 3-ketoacyl-CoA synthase (KCS), fatty acid hydroxylase (WAX2) and lipid transport proteins, validated the upregulation pattern in A<sub>t1</sub> compared with both A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig5">5f,g</a>). Large sequence variations existed between A<sub>t1</sub> and A<sub>1</sub> or A<sub>t1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> in the upstream or downstream regions of all of these genes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM1">3</a>). We introduced <i>KCS6</i>, a key gene in very-long-chain fatty acid biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Qin, Y. M. et al. Saturated very-long-chain fatty acids promote cotton fiber and Arabidopsis cell elongation by activating ethylene biosynthesis. Plant Cell 19, 3692–3704 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2108">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Xiao, G. H., Wang, K., Huang, G. &amp; Zhu, Y. X. Genome-scale analysis of the cotton KCS gene family revealed a binary mode of action for gibberellin A regulated fiber growth. J. Integr. Plant Biol. 58, 577–589 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2111">34</a></sup>, in <i>G. hirsutum</i> cv. Zhong24 background and observed a significant increase (~6.0–11.66%) of final fiber lengths in three homozygous transgenic lines (L241-1, L241-2, L241-3) that were driven by 35S promoter and one line (L245-1) driven by the fiber-specific E6 promoter (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig5">5h</a>). Fifty-six transcription factors, including WRKY12, HD-Zip2 and MYB6, showed differential expression patterns among the three cotton species that can be correlated with SVs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig5">5i</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM3">12</a>). In combination with genome scanning of transcription factor binding sites and A<sub>2</sub>–A<sub>t1</sub> differential expression, we identified 198 potential target genes for WRKY12 and 232 for HD-Zip2 in the cotton genome (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM3">13</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM3">14</a>). We suggest that higher expression intensities of these potential target genes in (AD)<sub>1</sub> may lead to longer fibers in (AD)<sub>1</sub> than in either A<sub>1</sub> or A<sub>2</sub>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="SV analysis among At1, A1 and A2."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: SV analysis among A<sub>t1</sub>, A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0607-4/MediaObjects/41588_2020_607_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0607-4/MediaObjects/41588_2020_607_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="754" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Comparisons of fiber elongation patterns. The center line in each box indicates the median, and the box limits indicate the upper and lower quartiles (<i>n</i> = 30 seeds). <b>b</b>, SVs of two A-genomes compared with the A<sub>t1</sub>-subgenome. <b>c</b>, Annotation of identified common SVs in genic regions. Up-/downstream, 5 kb regions from the start or stop codons. <b>d</b>, Volcano plots for A<sub>2</sub>~A<sub>t1</sub> gene expression in elongating fibers at 15 DPA. Each hollow point represents a gene and genes with SVs within 5 kb of their start or stop codons are indicated by a triangle. Dashed lines show the thresholds (<i>P</i> ≤ 0.001 and twofold change between A<sub>2</sub> and A<sub>t1</sub>). <b>e</b>, Gene ontology enrichment of significant differentially expressed genes with SVs (<i>P</i> ≤ 0.01). <b>f</b>, Upregulated genes in fatty acid biosynthetic process. Red items, upregulated genes in A<sub>t1</sub> relative to A<sub>2</sub> at 15 DPA. <b>g</b>, RT–qPCR analysis of upregulated genes in fatty acid biosynthetic pathway in elongating fibers at 5–20 DPA. <i>UBQ7</i> was used as a normalization control (mean ± s.d, <i>n</i> = 3 independent experiments). <b>h</b>, Cotton fibers of the WT (<i>G. hirsutum</i> cv. Zhong24) and the transgenic lines expressing <i>KCS6</i> gene under control of the CaMV 35S promoter (L241-1, L241-2 and L241-3) or E6 promoter (L245-1). The averaged fiber lengths with standard errors are denoted under each cotton line using Student’s <i>t</i>-test. Scale bar, 5 mm. <b>i</b>, RT–qPCR analysis of three upregulated potential transcription factor genes in elongating fibers at 5–20 DPA (mean ± s.d., <i>n</i> = 3 independent experiments). WT, wild type.</p><p><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM5">Source data</a></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>With high-quality assembly of two African–Asian species, A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub>, we provided a more complete landscape of genome architecture, gene annotations and TE insertions, which is critical to evolutionary and comparative genomics as well as genetic variation analysis. Our data suggested that A<sub>t</sub> may have originated from a primitive A-genome common ancestor, referred to here as A<sub>0</sub>, instead of extant A<sub>1</sub> or A<sub>2</sub>. Allotetraploid formation preceded the speciation of the present two A-genomes, and then A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> originated independently with no ancestor–progeny relations. Upon publication of our new data, we anticipate that reviews and textbooks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Hutchinson, J. B., Silow, R. A. &amp; Stephens, S. G. (eds) The Evolution of Gossypium and the Differentiation of the Cultivated Cottons (Oxford Univ. Press, 1947)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2264">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Wendel, J. F., Brubaker, C., Alvarez, I., Cronn, R. &amp; Stewart, J. M. Genetics and Genomics of Cotton Vol. 3 (ed. Paterson, A. H.) Ch. 1 (Springer, 2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2267">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Wendel, J.
