F. et al. Polyploidy and Genome Evolution (eds Soltis, P. S. &amp; Soltis, D. E.) Ch. 10 (Springer, 2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2270">35</a></sup> related to cotton genome evolution will have to be revisited and revised.</p><p>Several LTR bursts contributed compellingly to A-genome size expansion, speciation and evolution. By using fragmented coding sequences of LTRs, our GPDF analysis overcame a major pitfall related to most previous similar studies that relied on the presence of both ends of full-length LTRs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Hu, Y. et al. Gossypium barbadense and Gossypium hirsutum genomes provide insights into the origin and evolution of allotetraploid cotton. Nat. Genet. 51, 739–748 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2277">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat. Genet. 46, 567–572 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2280">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Ling, H. Q. et al. Genome sequence of the progenitor of wheat A subgenome Triticum urartu. Nature 557, 424–428 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2283">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Banks, J. A. et al. The Selaginella genome identifies genetic changes associated with the evolution of vascular plants. Science 332, 960–963 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2286">37</a></sup>, such that more recently inserted LTRs are likely over-represented. We suggest that GPDF may be applied to analyze accurately the time of LTR bursts and genome evolution. Analysis of SV and gene expression patterns identified putative candidates to investigate the phenotypic difference among three cotton species. These candidate genes would enable cotton breeders to further improve major agronomical traits such as fiber quality and yield.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Sampling and sequencing</h3><p>Genomic DNA molecules of <i>G. herbaceum</i> (var. <i>africanum</i> Mutema, A1-0076), <i>G</i>. <i>arboreum</i> (cv. Shixiya1) and <i>G. hir</i>s<i>utum</i> (TM-1) were isolated from young leaves of individual plants. We obtained polymerase reads of ~225 Gb, ~177 Gb and ~205 Gb from SMRT cells on PacBio RSII and Sequel instruments for <i>G. herbaceum</i> (A<sub>1</sub>), <i>G</i>. <i>arboreum</i> (A<sub>2</sub>) and <i>G. hir</i>s<i>utum</i> (AD)<sub>1</sub>, respectively. Our previously released ~133-Gb PacBio reads from A<sub>2</sub> were also integrated into our current A<sub>2</sub>-genome assembly. For A<sub>1</sub>-, A<sub>2</sub>- and (AD)<sub>1</sub>-genomes, we also obtained ~52 Gb, 95 Gb and 70 Gb of raw reads, respectively, with 400-bp inserts using a whole-genome shotgun approach on the Illumina HiSeq X-Ten platform. We sequenced ~256 Gb of clean Hi-C data for A<sub>1</sub>, ~219 Gb of clean Hi-C data for A<sub>2</sub> and ~196 Gb of clean Hi-C data for (AD)<sub>1</sub> on the Illumina HiSeq platform.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Assembly and correction</h3><p>We performed de novo assembly of PacBio long reads into contigs with the program Falcon (v.0.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050–1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2368">38</a></sup>. To further improve the accuracy of reference assembled contigs, two-step polishing strategies were performed: we first used PacBio long reads and carried out an initial polishing with Polish software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods 10, 563–569 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2372">39</a></sup> and then used highly accurate Illumina paired-end reads to further correct the assembly with Pilon (v.1.20) software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2376">40</a></sup>. The PacBio contigs were further clustered and extended into pseudo-chromosomes using Hi-C data. Gaps that existed in the genomes were filled using Pbjelly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="English, A. C. et al. Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with Pacific Biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS ONE 7, e47768 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2380">41</a></sup>, followed by a second round of polishing using Quiver<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods 10, 563–569 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2384">39</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Repeat analysis</h3><p>Each of the whole genomes was searched for repetitive sequences including tandem repeats and TEs. Tandem repeats were annotated by TRF (v.4.07b)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2396">42</a></sup> with the following parameters: 2, 7, 7, 80, 10, 50, 2,000. TE annotations were identified using a combination of de novo and homology-based approaches. A de novo repeat library was constructed with RepeatModeler (v.1.0.8). We adapted RepeatMasker (v.4.0.6)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Tarailo-Graovac, M. &amp; Chen, N. Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics 25, 4.10.1–4.10.14 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2400">43</a></sup> to search for similar TEs against Repbase (Repbase21.08)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Kapitonov, V. V. &amp; Jurka, J. A universal classification of eukaryotic transposable elements implemented in Repbase. Nat. Rev. Genet. 9, 411–412 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2404">44</a></sup>, mips-REdat library and the de novo repeat library. The RepeatProteinMask program was used to search against a TE protein database.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Analysis of potential LTR bursts using fragmented Gypsy-type transposons derived from full-length sequences</h3><p>Intact LTR retrotransposons were detected using LTR_FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265–W268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2416">45</a></sup> and classified into 64 families with 5′-LTR sequences based on the following parameters: similarity ≥ 80%, coverage ≥ 80% and copy number ≥ 100. A total of 13,332 LTR retrotransposons were translated in six frames that produced 1,397 Gypsy sequences with amino acids &gt; 1,000.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">GPDF fitting of LTR identity distributions and LTR burst time calculations</h3><p>Full-length and truncated LTRs were identified across genomes with various lengths and identities, and then each sequence (length = <i>l</i>) was divided into 30-bp units to determine the number of dots (<i>n</i> = <i>l</i>/30) with the same identity. Each Gypsy superfamily sequence was normalized to dot arrays with various identities, and all dot arrays were used to generate a box-plot according to their identities. For GPDF fitting and burst time calculation, single peaks in the TEs identity distribution curves were separated and fitted by GPDF with high adjusted <i>R</i><sup>2</sup> values, and the average nucleotide substitution ratio (<i>K</i>) was defined as 2.58 standard deviations (<i>σ</i>). Then the TE burst time point for individual amplification peaks was estimated by <i>t</i> = <i>K</i>/<i>r</i>, in which <i>r</i> is the nucleotide substitution rate for cotton species (<i>r</i> = 7 × 10<sup>−9</sup>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat. Genet. 46, 567–572 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2467">17</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Gene prediction and annotation</h3><p>Homology-based prediction, RNA-sequencing-assisted prediction and ab initio prediction were used for gene model prediction. For homology-based prediction, GeMoMa software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Keilwagen, J. et al. Using intron position conservation for homology-based gene prediction. Nucleic Acids Res. 44, e89 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2479">46</a></sup> was applied based on homologous proteins from sequenced species, which included <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (TAIR10, <a href="http://www.arabidopsis.org/">http://www.arabidopsis.org/</a>), <i>Oryza sativa</i> (v7.0), <i>G</i>. <i>arboreum</i>, <i>G</i>. <i>hirsutum</i>, <i>G</i>. <i>raimondii</i> (D<sub>5</sub>), <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> (v.3.1), <i>T. cacao</i> (<a href="http://cocoa-genome-hub.southgreen.fr">http://cocoa-genome-hub.southgreen.fr</a>) and <i>Vitis vinifera</i> (Genoscope 12×). RNA sequencing transcripts assembled with HISAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. Methods 12, 357–360 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2528">47</a></sup> and StringTie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 290–295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2532">48</a></sup> were used to assist in gene structure predictions (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM1">15</a>). In summary, a total of 52,444 (mean size: 2,177.9 bp), 56,130 (mean size: 2,414.7 bp) and 111,872 (mean size: 1,892.1 bp) assembled transcripts were obtained for A<sub>1</sub>, A<sub>2</sub> and (AD)<sub>1</sub>, respectively. For ab initio gene prediction, we applied SNAP (V2006-07-28)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatic 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2546">49</a></sup>, Augustus (v.3.2.2)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W435–W439 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2550">50</a></sup>, Genscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Burge, C. &amp; Karlin, S. Prediction of complete gene structures in human genomic DNA. J. Mol. Biol. 268, 78–94 (1997)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2555">51</a></sup> and GlimmerHMM (v.3.0.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Majoros, W. H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two open source ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878–2879 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2559">52</a></sup> to generate gene structures. Finally, all predictions were integrated to produce a consensus gene set using EVidenceModeler (v.1.1.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2563">53</a></sup>. Gene functional annotations were assigned by aligning protein sequences to Swiss-Prot and TrEMB<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Bateman, A. et al. UniProt: a hub for protein information. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D204–D212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2567">54</a></sup> using BLASTP (<i>E</i> value (expected value) ≤ 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>), KAAS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Moriya, Y., Itoh, M., Okuda, S., Yoshizawa, A. C. &amp; Kanehisa, M. KAAS: an automatic genome annotation and pathway reconstruction server. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W182–W185 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2577">55</a></sup> (v.2.1) and InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236–1240 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2581">56</a></sup> (v.5.24). Gene Ontology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Ashburner, M. et al. Gene Ontology: tool for the unification of biology. Nat. Genet. 25, 25–29 (2000)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2585">57</a></sup> IDs for each gene were extracted from the InterPro entry.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>We used BLASTP to generate protein sequence pairs (<i>E</i> value ≤ 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>) and then OrthoMCL (v.2.0.9)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2602">58</a></sup> to cluster gene families with an inflation value of 1.5. The single-copy gene families were extracted and aligned using MAFFT (v.7.058)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Katoh, K. &amp; Standley, D. M. MAFFT multiple sequence alignment software version 7: improvements in performance and usability. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 772–780 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2606">59</a></sup>. A phylogenetic tree was constructed using a maximum likelihood method implemented in RAxML (v.8.0.19)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2610">60</a></sup> with a GTRGAMMA substitution model with <i>O. sativa</i> as the outgroup. The Markov chain Monte Carlo algorithm for Bayes estimation was adopted to calculate the divergence time using PAML (v.4.6)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Yang, Z. PAML: a program package for phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Comput. Appl. Biosci. 13, 555–556 (1997)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2618">61</a></sup>. For the identification of SNPs on orthologous genes among A<sub>1</sub>, A<sub>2</sub>, A<sub>t1</sub> in (AD)<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>t2</sub> in (AD)<sub>2</sub> (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Wang, M. et al. Reference genome sequences of two cultivated allotetraploid cottons, Gossypium hirsutum and Gossypium barbadense. Nat. Genet. 51, 224–229 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2635">21</a></sup>), we used BLASTP to do pairwise alignments and retained only homologous gene pairs with reciprocal best hits (<i>E</i> value ≤ 1 × 10<sup>−5</sup>). Then we generated multiple alignments of homologous proteins and back-translated to the CDS. A SNP was determined to be present if a position in the alignment included two or more different bases. If a SNP was identified in the aligned CDS but no resulting amino acid variation occurred in the corresponding position of alignment, this site was defined as an identical site within the ortholog. To further understand phylogenetic relationships among A<sub>1</sub>, A<sub>2</sub> and A<sub>t1</sub>, we focused on specific recombination regions to infer gene trees according to a previous report<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Pease, J. B. &amp; Hahn, M. W. More accurate phylogenies inferred from low-recombination regions in the presence of incomplete lineage sorting. Evolution 67, 2376–2384 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2651">62</a></sup>. We applied reported methods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Schumer, M., Cui, R., Powell, D. L., Rosenthal, G. G. &amp; Andolfatto, P. Ancient hybridization and genomic stabilization in a swordtail fish. Mol. Ecol. 25, 2661–2679 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2655">63</a></sup> to further perform phylogenetic analysis among A<sub>1</sub>, A<sub>2</sub> and A<sub>t1</sub> or A<sub>t2</sub>. In brief, genome alignments were divided into 10-kb segments and we performed an approximately unbiased test. The site likelihoods for each possible topology were calculated by RAXML, then these likelihoods were input into Consel<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Shimodaira, H. &amp; Hasegawa, M. CONSEL: for assessing the confidence of phylogenetic tree selection. Bioinformatics 17, 1246–1247 (2001)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2668">64</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">SNP identification</h3><p>The sequenced reads of 14 A<sub>1</sub> and 67 A<sub>2</sub> cotton accessions were mapped to our assembled A<sub>2</sub>-genome in this study using BWA (0.7.10-r789)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows–Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2686">65</a></sup>. PCR duplications in the alignments were removed in Picard (v.1.94). SNPs and indels identified by the HaplotypeCaller module were then used to perform base-quality recalibration with the BaseRecalibrator and IndelRealigner modules in the GATK toolkit (v.3.8)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="McKenna, A. et al. The genome analysis toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297–1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2690">66</a></sup>. The genomic variants in GVCF (genomic variant call format) for each accession as identified by the HaplotypeCaller module and the GVCF files were merged. Raw SNP calls were further filtered using GATK filter expressions (‘QUAL&lt;30.0||QD&lt;2.0||FS&gt;60.0||MQ&lt;40.0||SOR&gt;4.0’ --clusterWindowSize 5 --clusterSize 2).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Population genetics analysis</h3><p>A subset of 9,555,165 SNPs (max-missing &gt; 0.5, minor allele frequency &gt; 0.05) in the 14 A<sub>1</sub> and 67 A<sub>2</sub> cotton accessions was screened to build a neighbor-joining tree in MEGA7 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G. &amp; Tamura, K. MEGA7: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis version 7.0 for bigger datasets. Mol. Biol. Evol. 33, 1870–1874 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2706">67</a></sup>) with 1,000 bootstrap replicates using D<sub>5</sub> as the outgroup. The cotton population structure analysis and a PCA were carried out using admixture<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Alexander, D. H., Novembre, J. &amp; Lange, K. Fast model-based estimation of ancestry in unrelated individuals. Genome Res. 19, 1655–1664 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2712">68</a></sup> with <i>K</i> values from 2 to 3 and EIGENSOFT software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Price, A. L. et al. Principal components analysis corrects for stratification in genome-wide association studies. Nat. Genet. 38, 904–909 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2720">69</a></sup>, respectively. A pairwise fixation statistic (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>) analysis as calculated in the PopGenome package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Pfeifer, B., Wittelsbürger, U., Ramos-Onsins, S. E. &amp; Lercher, M. J. PopGenome: an efficient Swiss army knife for population genomic analyses in R. Mol. Biol. Evol. 31, 1929–1936 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2728">70</a></sup> was used to estimate the degree of variability in three groups (A<sub>1</sub> accessions worldwide, A<sub>2</sub> accessions from the India and Pakistan group, and A<sub>2</sub> accessions from the China group). To validate the relationships of the A<sub>1</sub>, A<sub>2</sub> and A<sub>t1</sub>, we used 30 released (AD)<sub>1</sub> accessions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Fang, L. et al. Genomic analyses in cotton identify signatures of selection and loci associated with fiber quality and yield traits. Nat. Genet. 49, 1089–1098 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2748">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Wang, M. et al. Asymmetric subgenome selection and cis-regulatory divergence during cotton domestication. Nat. Genet. 49, 579–587 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2751">71</a></sup>, 21 released A<sub>2</sub> accessions and 14 released A<sub>1</sub> accessions to construct a population phylogenetic tree with D<sub>5</sub> as the outgroup (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#Fig12">7</a>). The identification of ancestral alleles was as described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Du, X. et al. Resequencing of 243 diploid cotton accessions based on an updated A genome identifies the genetic basis of key agronomic traits. Nat. Genet. 50, 796–802 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2764">18</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Phylogenetic weighting</h3><p>For genome-wide evaluation of three possible phylogenetic hypotheses, a method called Twisst<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Martin, S. H. &amp; Van Belleghem, S. M. Exploring evolutionary relationships across the genome using topology weighting. Genetics 206, 429–438 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2776">72</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Van Belleghem, S. M. et al. Complex modular architecture around a simple toolkit of wing pattern genes. Nat. Ecol. Evol. 1, 52 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2779">73</a></sup> was applied to analyze A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> accessions. In brief, the phasing and imputation of filtered SNPs (minAlleles 2, depth (DP) ≥ 5, genotype quality (GQ) ≥ 30) obtained from the 14 A<sub>1</sub> and 67 A<sub>2</sub> accessions and the outgroup D<sub>5</sub> were performed using Beagle software with default parameters. Trees were constructed for each sliding window of 50 SNPs across 13 chromosomes using Phyml software, then tree weightings were computed using Twisst, with four defined taxa: D<sub>5</sub>, A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> from China, and from India/Pakistan.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Demographic analysis</h3><p>The G-PhoCS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Gronau, I., Hubisz, M. J., Gulko, B., Danko, C. G. &amp; Siepel, A. Bayesian inference of ancient human demography from individual genome sequences. Nat. Genet. 43, 1031–1034 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2809">74</a></sup> method was employed to infer the complete demographic history for A<sub>1</sub>, A<sub>2</sub> and A<sub>t1</sub> based on 2,468 selected neutral loci. Coalescence simulations were run under two models, M1 (no gene flow) and M2 (ancient gene flow). To further convert estimates of divergence time (<i>τ</i>) and population size (<i>θ</i>) from mutations per site to years (<i>T</i>) and effective numbers of individuals (<i>N</i><sub>e</sub>), respectively, we assumed an A<sub>1</sub>A<sub>2</sub>–A<sub>t1</sub> average genomic divergence time of <i>T</i><sub>div</sub> ≈ 1.0 Ma (0.6–1.8 Ma), which was calculated by the molecular tree based on single-copy genes, and an annual production. We further applied <i>fastsimcoal2</i> software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Excoffier, L., Dupanloup, I., Huerta-Sanchez, E., Sousa, V. C. &amp; Foll, M. Robust demographic inference from genomic and SNP data. PLoS Genet. 9, e1003905 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2847">75</a></sup> to infer demographic history based on fourfold degenerate sites selected from SNP datasets (minor allele frequencies &gt; 0.05) from 30 released (AD)<sub>1</sub> accessions, 21 released A<sub>2</sub> accessions and 14 released A<sub>1</sub> accessions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">SVs among three cotton genomes</h3><p>SVs were identified using NGMLR (v.0.2.4)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Fritz, J. S. et al. Accurate detection of complex structural variations using single-molecule sequencing. Nat. Methods 15, 461–468 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d31216e2866">76</a></sup> and PbSV (v.0.1.0). First, we mapped the PacBio subreads of A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> to the genome of (AD)<sub>1</sub> using NGMLR with default parameters, and then PbSV was used to find large indels with length &gt;50 bp using parameters: gapdistance = 1,000, min_readcount = 2, min_readfraction = 0.2, positionwiggle = 200, basepairidwiggle = 0.25, call_min_mapq = 10.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">RT–qPCR analysis and plant transformation</h3><p>Total RNA (~2 μg) was extracted and was then reverse transcribed in a 20-μl reaction mixture with TransScript cDNA Synthesis SuperMix (TransGen Biotech). Then 1-μl sample aliquots were used as templates for RT–qPCR analysis. <i>UBQ7</i> was used as the internal control for RT–qPCR data analysis. The CDS sequences of the <i>KCS6</i> gene were PCR amplified from the complementary DNA of 10-DPA fiber tissue and cloned into the pCAMBIA2300 vector, forming <i>35S::KCS6</i> or <i>E6::KCS6</i> constructs. Then the construct was introduced into <i>Agrobacterium tumefaciens</i> strain LBA4404, and subsequently transferred into the Upland cotton <i>G. hirsutum</i> cv. Zhong24. All primers used in this study are presented in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM1">16</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Statistical analyses</h3><p>Student’s two-tailed <i>t</i>-tests were performed in GraphPad Prism software.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Reporting Summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM2">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The genome sequence data for A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> are deposited in NCBI (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA506494">PRJNA506494</a>). The (AD)<sub>1</sub>-genome sequence data are accessible through NCBI (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA524970">PRJNA524970</a>). The assemblies and annotation files of A<sub>1</sub>, A<sub>2</sub> and (AD)<sub>1</sub> are available at the CottonGen website (<a href="https://www.cottongen.org/">https://www.cottongen.org/</a>). The re-sequence data for A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> accessions can be accessed with accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA507537">PRJNA507537</a> in NCBI. Source data for Figs. 2 and 5 and Extended Data Fig. 5 are presented with the paper.</p>

Science 335, 1148 (2012)." /><span class="c-article-references__counter">5.</span><p class="c-article-references__text" id="ref-CR5">Zahn, L. M. Unraveling the origin of cotton. <i>Science</i> <b>335</b>, 1148 (2012).</p><p class="c-article-references__links u-hide-print"><a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" aria-label="Search for reference 5 on Google Scholar" href="http://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?&amp;title=Unraveling%20the%20origin%20of%20cotton&amp;journal=Science&amp;volume=335&amp;publication_year=2012&amp;author=Zahn%2CLM">

                </p></li><li class="c-article-references__item js-c-reading-companion-references-item" itemprop="citation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle"><meta itemprop="author" content="SG. G.H., K.W., Y.L., J.Z.Y., R.G.P. and J.E.F. prepared the samples and contributed to data analysis. G.H. and Z.W. performed the bioinformatics work. The upper heatmap shows a chromatin interaction matrix that maps Hi-C data from A<sub>2</sub> against the A<sub>2</sub>-genome (A<sub>2</sub> map to A<sub>2</sub>), and maps Hi-C data from A<sub>1</sub> against the A<sub>2</sub>-genome (A<sub>1</sub> map to A<sub>2</sub>). The middle panel shows a diagram of the inversion region with the four red dots representing the inversion borders. The lower heatmap shows a chromatin interaction matrix that maps Hi-C data from A<sub>1</sub> against the A<sub>1</sub>-genome (A<sub>1</sub> map to A<sub>1</sub>) and maps Hi-C data from A<sub>2</sub> against the A<sub>1</sub>-genome (A<sub>2</sub> map to A<sub>1</sub>). <b>c</b>, <b>d</b>, Validation of inversion borders in chromosomes 10 (<b>c</b>) and 12 (<b>d</b>) by PCR amplification. The unprocessed gel for the cropped images are presented in source data.
                          <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM5">Source data</a>
                        </p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig11"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4/figures/11" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0607-4/MediaObjects/41588_2020_607_Fig11_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 6 Phylogenetic relationship among A<sub>1</sub>, A<sub>2</sub> and A-subgenome.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Distribution of mean recombination rates in protein-coding genes with available recombination rates (n = 240 genes). <b>b</b>, Proportion of genes with 0-20% and 80-100% of recombination rates supporting the three trees. <b>c</b>, Summary of phylogenetic analysis with the AU test in 10 kb windows among A<sub>1</sub>, A<sub>2</sub>, A<sub>t2</sub>, D<sub>5</sub>.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig12"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4/figures/12" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0607-4/MediaObjects/41588_2020_607_Fig12_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 7 A phylogenetic tree based on SNPs in the genomes with D<sub>5</sub>-genome as the outgroup.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Units (as measured by the indicated scale) show the percentage of represented polymorphic sites that differed between two individuals. Detail information of cotton accessions were described in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4#MOESM1">4</a>.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig13"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4/figures/13" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0607-4/MediaObjects/41588_2020_607_Fig13_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 8 Topology weighting for A<sub>1</sub> and A<sub>2</sub> populations.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, Three possible taxon topologies for A<sub>1</sub>, A<sub>2</sub> from China group (CHG) and A<sub>2</sub> mostly from India and Pakistan group (IPG) with D<sub>5</sub> as the outgroup. <b>b-n</b>, Weightings for all three topologies plotted across the 13 chromosomes. Colour for each topology corresponds to coloring in <b>a</b>.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig14"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4/figures/14" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0607-4/MediaObjects/41588_2020_607_Fig14_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 9 The burst of LTR/Gypsy amplification fits a Gaussian distribution and allows time estimation of burst events.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>-<b>e</b>, GPDF modeling data fit well with the actual TE bursts found in A<sub>1</sub> with R<sup>2</sup> from 0.86–0.99 (<b>a</b>), A<sub>2</sub> with R<sup>2</sup> from 0.86–0.98 (<b>b</b>), D<sub>5</sub> with R<sup>2</sup>=0.94 (<b>c</b>), A<sub>t1</sub> with R<sup>2</sup> from 0.95–0.99 (<b>d</b>) and D<sub>t1</sub> with R<sup>2</sup> from 0.91–0.93 (<b>e</b>). The estimated time of the LTR/Gypsy burst event associated with each genome or subgenome is shown in the graphs. The peak width was defined as 2.58σ that covers &gt;99.5% of the nucleotide substitution events in TEs.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig15"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-020-0607-4/figures/15" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0607-4/MediaObjects/41588_2020_607_Fig15_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 10 Volcano plots for A<sub>1</sub>-A<sub>t1</sub> gene expression in elongating fibers at 15 DPA.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Each hollow point represents a gene and genes with SVs within 5 kb of their start or stop codons are indicated by a triangle. The dashed lines show the thresholds (<i>P</i>-value ≤ 0.001 and two-fold change).</p></div></div></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec28" data-title="Supplementary information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec28-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec28">Supplementary information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec28-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info">
